Uthman's Qualifications and the Contract by Abd al-Rahman
Establishes Uthman's imamate by conditions and two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Uthman's possession of the conditions of imamate, together with the valid contract by Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf and his inclusion in the shura council, establish the legitimacy of his caliphate.

Core Idea
Uthman's caliphate is proven by objective qualifications and a valid contract, not mere politics.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi presents the Sunni argument for Uthman's caliphate. It begins by establishing that Uthman possessed all the necessary conditions for imamate, then provides two key proofs: the contract by Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf and his inclusion in the shura council by Umar. These arguments demonstrate that Uthman's election was based on merit and collective agreement.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditions of imamate
Read the first part where the author lists the conditions: lineage, knowledge, asceticism, ability. Notice that these are the same conditions applicable to any imam.
These conditions are standard in Sunni theology.
2
Examine the contract by Abd al-Rahman
The passage argues that Abd al-Rahman, being qualified, contracted for Uthman. If Uthman were unqualified, Abd al-Rahman would not have done so. Consider the logic of this proof.
This is a rational argument from the action of a qualified person.
3
Consider the shura council inclusion
Umar included Uthman in the shura council. The Companions did not object, which indicates Uthman's qualification. This is a proof from consensus and the practice of the Companions.
The lack of objection from the Companions is significant in Sunni thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إمامة عثمان وإذا ثبت إمامة الشيخين - رضي الله عنهما - بما ذكرنا من الدلائل ثبتت إمامة عثمان، لأن جميع شرائط الإمامة من النسب والعلم والزهد والقدرة على القيام بجميع ما يُحتاج إلى الإمام لأجله ثابتة في حقه، وقد عقد له عبد الرحمن بن عوف الخلافة وهو أهل للعقد بدليل اشتغال عبد الرحمن بن عوف بالعقد له ولو لم يكن أهلاً لما فعل، ولأنه أحد أهل الشورى، ولو لم يكن أهلاً للخلافة لما أدخله عمر في أهل الشورى، ولو فعل ذلك لأنكره الصحابة إذ هم الموصوفون بتغيير المنكر، وحيث لم ينكروا، دل أنه كان أهلاً لذلك، ولأنه وجد فيه من شرائط الإمامة من النسب، والعلم بالحلال والحرام، وحفظ القرآن والعدالة والاستقلال بكفاية ما يناط بالإمام، وشرعة الخلافة له كانت ثابتة، وإذا ثبت ذلك ثم عقد من هو أهل للعقد ينعقد ضرورة، كيف وقد انضم إلى عقد عبد الرحمن بن عوف إجماع الصحابة - رضي الله عنهم - وهو دليل موجب للعلم قطعا ويقينا، وما روى أن عبد الرحمن قال لعلي - رضي الله عنهما – أوليك على أن تحكم بكتاب الله وسنة رسوله وسيرة الشيخين فقال علي: أحكم بكتاب الله
Discussion on the imamate of Uthman. If the imamate of the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar) - may Allah be pleased with them - is established by the proofs we have mentioned, then the imamate of Uthman is established, because all the conditions of imamate – lineage, knowledge, asceticism, and ability to fulfill everything needed from an imam – are established in his case. Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf contracted the caliphate for him, and he (Abd al-Rahman) was qualified to contract, as evidenced by his undertaking the contract for him; if he had not been qualified, he would not have done it. Also, because he (Uthman) was one of the people of the shura (council); if he had not been qualified for the caliphate, Umar would not have included him among the people of the shura, and if he had done that, the Companions would have objected, for they are described as forbidding wrong. Since they did not object, it indicates that he was qualified for that. Also, because the conditions of imamate – lineage, knowledge of the lawful and unlawful, memorization of the Quran, justice, and independence sufficient for what is entrusted to the imam – were found in him, and the legality of the caliphate for him was established. Once that is established, then a contract by one who is qualified to contract necessarily takes effect. Moreover, the contract of Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf was joined by the consensus of the Companions – may Allah be pleased with them – which is a proof that necessitates certainty conclusively. And it is narrated that Abd al-Rahman said to Ali – may Allah be pleased with them both – 'I give you the pledge on condition that you judge by the Book of Allah, the Sunnah of His Messenger, and the conduct of the two shaykhs,' and Ali said: 'I judge by the Book of Allah...'
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage is a structured proof for the legitimacy of Uthman's caliphate. The author begins by stating the topic: 'Discussion on the imamate of Uthman.' He then argues that since the imamate of Abu Bakr and Umar has been proven, Uthman's imamate follows because he possesses all the necessary conditions: lineage (being from Quraysh), knowledge, asceticism, and ability to fulfill the duties of an imam. The author then provides four specific proofs: First, Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, who was qualified to contract the caliphate, contracted it for Uthman; if Uthman were unqualified, Abd al-Rahman would not have done so. Second, Umar ibn al-Khattab included Uthman among the six-member shura council; if Uthman were unqualified, Umar would not have included him, and the other Companions would have objected—but they did not, proving his qualification. Third, Uthman possessed all the conditions of imamate: lineage, knowledge of halal and haram, memorization of the Quran, justice, and independence in handling the affairs of the imamate. Fourth, after establishing these qualifications, the contract by a qualified person (Abd al-Rahman) necessarily takes effect. Moreover, this contract was accompanied by the consensus of the Companions, which yields certain knowledge. The author then references the well-known narration where Abd al-Rahman offered the caliphate to Ali on condition that he rule by the Book, Sunnah, and the conduct of Abu Bakr and Umar, and Ali replied 'I judge by the Book of Allah...' — implying that Ali accepted the condition, which also implicitly acknowledges the legitimacy of the previous caliphs. The argument is cumulative: conditions + qualified contract + consensus = certainty.

Key Terms
الإمامة
أهل الشورى
عبد الرحمن بن عوف
شرائط الإمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Uthman became caliph only because of his relationship to the Prophet or due to political maneuvering.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that Uthman's caliphate was based on his possession of the necessary conditions (lineage, knowledge, etc.) and the deliberate actions of qualified individuals (Abd al-Rahman's contract and Umar's shura inclusion), all of which received tacit consensus from the Companions.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition of imamate mentioned in the passage?
A) lineage
B) asceticism
C) wealth
D) ability

Key Takeaway
Uthman's caliphate is legitimized by his fulfillment of the conditions of imamate and by the actions of Abd al-Rahman and Umar, which imply his qualification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate the leaders in your own life, do you apply the same objective criteria—qualification, knowledge, capability—that the Companions used, or do you rely on popularity or personal benefit? What would it take for you to recognize legitimate authority based on merit, even if it's unpopular?
?
If you had been among the Companions and witnessed Umar including Uthman in the shura, would you have remained silent like they did? What does your answer reveal about your trust in the judgment of qualified people and your willingness to defer to their decisions?
?
The Companions are described as those who forbid wrong—they would have objected if something was wrong. In your own life, are you quick to object to perceived injustices, or do you remain silent when you see something questionable? How does this lesson challenge your silence and call you to action?

The Consensus of the Companions and the Pledge of Ali
Completes proof with consensus and narration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus of the Companions and the narration about Ali's conditional pledge further confirm Uthman's caliphate.

Core Idea
Consensus of the Companions yields certain knowledge of Uthman's caliphate.

The previous lesson established Uthman's qualifications through conditions and specific actions. This lesson completes the argument by showing that the contract of Abd al-Rahman was joined by the consensus of all Companions, making the imamate a matter of certainty. The narration about Ali's conditional pledge also implicitly recognizes Uthman's legitimacy.

Guided Path
1
Review the third proof: Uthman's personal conditions
The passage again lists conditions: lineage, knowledge of halal/haram, Quran memorization, justice, independence. Note that these are the same as before but elaborated.
This reinforces the earlier claim.
2
Understand the necessity of the contract
Once qualifications are established, the contract by a qualified person necessarily takes effect. This is a logical conclusion.
The author emphasizes the necessity of the contract.
3
Appreciate the role of consensus
The contract was not just by Abd al-Rahman but was joined by the consensus of all Companions. This consensus is a proof that yields certain knowledge.
Consensus is a key source of knowledge in Sunni Islam.
4
Consider the narration about Ali
Abd al-Rahman offered the caliphate to Ali on condition that he rule by the Book, Sunnah, and conduct of Abu Bakr and Umar. Ali's response indicates acceptance, which also implies recognition of the previous caliphs.
This narration supports the legitimacy of the first three caliphs.

?
If the Companions' consensus is a proof that necessitates certainty, what does that mean for your own attachment to the way of the early Muslims? Are there any aspects of the sunna that you have been careless about, thinking they are not essential? What would it take for you to treat their consensus as decisive?
?
Ali's willingness to judge by the conduct of Abu Bakr and Umar shows his submission to the collective guidance of the community. Is there an area of your life where you resist the example of the righteous predecessors because it is difficult or uncomfortable? How can you humble yourself to follow their path?
?
The lesson lists conditions for leadership: lineage, knowledge, justice, memorization of Quran, independence. When you choose leaders or role models in your own life, what criteria do you use? Are you drawn more to worldly qualities than to these spiritual and moral ones? How can you realign your standards?

Proof of Uthman's Imamate from Conditions and Consensus
Author proves Uthman's imamate through conditions and consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author proves Uthman's imamate by establishing that he fulfilled all necessary conditions and that his appointment was confirmed by the consensus of the Companions.

Core Idea
Uthman's imamate is proven by his qualifications and the consensus of the Companions.

After establishing the imamate of Abu Bakr and Umar, the author turns to Uthman. He argues that all necessary conditions for leadership were present in Uthman, and that his selection by the shura committee and the subsequent consensus of the Companions provide an unassailable proof. This lesson walks through the logical steps of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get a sense of the overall argument. Notice the author's methodical style.
The author builds his case step by step.
2
Identify the conditions for imamate
List the conditions the author mentions: lineage, knowledge, asceticism, ability to fulfill the needs of the imamate. Note how he asserts they are all present in Uthman.
3
Trace the shura and consensus arguments
Observe the argument from the shura: Umar included Uthman, and no Companion objected, proving his qualification. Then see how Abd al-Rahman's contract combined with consensus yields certainty.
The author uses the silence of the Companions as evidence of approval.
4
Understand the role of consensus
The author concludes that the consensus of the Companions, added to the contract, provides certain knowledge. Reflect on why consensus is such a strong proof in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت إمامة الشيخين - رضي الله عنهما - بما ذكرنا من الدلائل ثبتت إمامة عثمان رضي الله عنه، لأن جميع شرائط الإمامة من النسب والعلم والزهد والقدرة على القيام بجميع ما يُحتاج إلى الإمام لأجله ثابتة في حقه، وقد عقد له عبد الرحمن بن عوف الخلافة وهو أهل للعقد بدليل اشتغال عبد الرحمن بن عوف له بالعقد له، ولو لم يكن أهلاً لما فعل، ولأنه أحد أهل الشورى، ولو لم يكن أهلاً للخلافة لما أدخله عمر رضي الله عنه في أهل الشورى، ولو فعل ذلك لأنكره الصحابة إذ هم الموصوفون بتغيير المنكر، وحيث لم ينكروا، دل أنه كان أهلاً لذلك، ولأن وجد في غيره من شرائط الإمامة من النسب، والعلم بالحلال والحرام، وحفظ القرآن والعدالة والاستقلال بكفاية ما يناط بالإمام، وشرعة الخلافة له كانت ثابتة، وإذا ثبت ذلك ثم عقد من هو أهل للعقد ينعقد ضرورة، كيف وقد انضم إلى عقد عبد الرحمن بن عوف إجماع الصحابة - رضي الله عنهم - وهو دليل موجب للعلم قطعا ويقينا.
And when the imamate of the two shaykhs (may Allah be pleased with them) is established by the proofs we have mentioned, the imamate of Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) is established, because all the conditions of imamate—lineage, knowledge, asceticism, and ability to undertake everything needed for the imam—are established in his right. And Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf contracted the caliphate for him, and he was qualified for the contract, evidenced by Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf undertaking the contract for him; if he were not qualified, he would not have done so. And because he was one of the people of the shura; if he were not qualified for the caliphate, Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) would not have included him among the people of the shura; and if he had done so, the Companions would have objected, for they are described as changing wrong, and since they did not object, it indicates that he was qualified for that. And because the other conditions of imamate—lineage, knowledge of the lawful and unlawful, memorization of the Quran, justice, and self-sufficiency in fulfilling what is entrusted to the imam—were found in him, and the way of the caliphate was established for him. And when this is established, then a contract made by one who is qualified for the contract necessarily concludes, especially since the consensus of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) was joined to the contract of Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, and that is a proof yielding certain knowledge definitively.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the imamate of Uthman ibn Affan. The author begins by stating that once the imamate of the first two caliphs is established, Uthman's follows because all the necessary conditions (lineage, knowledge, asceticism, ability) are present in him. Then he gives multiple arguments: first, Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, who was qualified, made the contract for him; if Uthman were unqualified, he would not have done so. Second, Umar included Uthman among the six of the shura; Umar's inclusion indicates qualification, and the Companions' silence in the face of any error confirms it. Third, all required qualities—noble lineage, knowledge of halal and haram, Quran memorization, justice, and capability—were found in Uthman. Since these are established, and the contract was made by a qualified person, the imamate is necessarily valid. The strongest proof is that the consensus of the Companions joined Abd al-Rahman's contract, which yields certain knowledge (qat'an wa yaqinan).

Key Terms
إمامة
شرائط الإمامة
إجماع
أهل الشورى
عقد الخلافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim Uthman's imamate was not based on consensus.
Correction: The passage demonstrates consensus through the shura selection and the lack of objection from the Companions, which the author treats as binding.

Checkpoint
What does the author present as the strongest proof for Uthman's imamate?
A) Umar's inclusion of Uthman in the shura
B) Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf's contract
C) The consensus of the Companions joining Abd al-Rahman's contract
D) Uthman's lineage and knowledge

Key Takeaway
Uthman's imamate is validated by the fulfillment of all imamate conditions and the definitive consensus of the Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a leader appointed by a process you don't fully understand, do you instinctively question their legitimacy or do you look for the signs of qualification and community acceptance—as the Companions did with Uthman? What does your reaction say about your trust in Allah's arrangement of affairs?
?
The Companions remained silent when Uthman was included in the shura, and that silence is treated as consent. Is your silence in the face of Islamic leadership decisions a form of consent, or does it come from indifference? How can you cultivate the same sense of responsibility they had to speak up if something were wrong?
?
Uthman met all the conditions of imamate—lineage, knowledge, asceticism, justice. If you were to be entrusted with a role of authority today, which of these conditions would you be weakest in, and what concrete step can you take this week to strengthen it so that you are not a burden but a blessing to the ummah?

Ali’s Response and the Ijtihad Principle
Narration and ijtihad methodology explanation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ali’s refusal to follow the sira of Abu Bakr and Umar does not indicate opposition but stems from a valid ijtihad methodology.

Core Idea
Ali’s ijtihad rule, not opposition, shaped his response.

This lesson examines a well-known report where Ali was offered the caliphate on condition of following the sira of Abu Bakr and Umar. The author uses this incident to defend the caliphs and clarify that Ali’s response was a matter of Islamic legal methodology, not a rejection of their authority.

Guided Path
1
Read the report and initial implication
Read the Arabic passage that narrates the exchange between Abd al-Rahman, Ali, and Uthman. Notice the author’s immediate conclusion that this report proves the soundness of the caliphate of Abu Bakr and Umar.
The report is a key historical incident used in theological debates.
2
Understand the ijtihad difference
Focus on the explanation that Ali’s condition to use his own ijtihad was because his madhhab prohibited taqlid for a mujtahid, while Abd al-Rahman and Uthman allowed it when the other is more knowledgeable. This is not opposition but a methodological disagreement.
3
Reflect on the continuity of this difference
Note that the author states this difference remained among later scholars, showing it is a legitimate area of ijtihad.
This contextualizes the incident within mainstream Islamic jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما روى أن عبد الرحمن قال لعلي - رضي الله عنهما - أبايعك على أن تحكم بكتاب الله وسنة رسوله وسيرة الشيخين فقال علي: أحكم بكتاب الله وسنة رسوله وأجتهد رأيي، فقال ذلك لعثمان، فقال: نعم. دليل على صحة خلافة الشيخين واعتقاد الصحابة بإمامتهما، وأنهم كانوا يحمدون طريقهما ويقتفون آثارهما ويسلكون سبيلهما ويرضون بسيرتهما. وقول علي رضي الله عنه: أحكم بكتاب الله وسنة رسوله عليه السلام وأجتهد رأيي، ليس بدليل على مخالفته لهما ومجانبته إياهما لما مر من الدلائل الدالة على متابعته إياهما ورضاه بإمامتهما، بل ذلك لأن مذهبه كان أن المجتهد يجب عليه اتباع اجتهاده، ولا يجوز له تقليد غير من المجتهدين، وكان مذهب عبد الرحمن بن عوف وعثمان أن المجتهد يجوز أن يقلد غيره إذا كان أفقه منه، وأعلم بطرق الدين وأبصر بوجوه المقاييس، وأن يترك اجتهاد نفسه ورأيه ويتبع رأي ذلك، وبقى هذا الخلاف في أئمة الدين وفقهاء الأمة. والدليل على أن ذلك كان لاختلاف فيما بينهما في هذه المسألة دون أن يرى على مخالفتهما: أنه بايع عثمان رضي الله عنه واعتقد إمامته، وكان يساعده في أموره. والحاصل أن كل من يأبى خلافة أحد من الخلفاء الراشدين أو ينسب واحدا منهم إلى ما لا يحل أو إلى ما يوجب قدحا في حاله، فهو ممن يزيل إجماع الصحابة عن كونه حجة عليه، ويصفهم بالإجماع على ما هو المنكر، والامتناع عن تغييره، وأكثر ما يروون ما يوجب قدحا في واحد منهم أخبار تثبت بطريق الآحاد، ومدار ذلك كله على من لا يوثق به فلا يكون مقبولاً بمقابلة إجماع الصحابة، وكثير مما يروون أشياء لا حجة لهم فيها، ويظنونها بجهالتهم بمخارجها ووجوهها حجة لهم، والتمسك بإجماع الصحابة أولى من اتباع مثل تلك الروايات الشاذة الخارجة عن
And what is narrated that Abd al-Rahman said to Ali (may Allah be pleased with them both): 'I pledge allegiance to you on condition that you rule by the Book of Allah, the Sunnah of His Messenger, and the sira of the two shaykhs,' so Ali said: 'I rule by the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of His Messenger and I exercise my own ijtihad,' then he said that to Uthman, and he said: 'Yes.' This is evidence for the soundness of the caliphate of the two shaykhs and the Companions' belief in their imamate, and that they used to praise their way, follow their traces, walk their path, and be pleased with their sira. And the statement of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'I rule by the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of His Messenger (peace be upon him) and I exercise my own ijtihad' is not evidence of his opposing them and distancing himself from them, because of the proofs that have passed indicating his following them and his satisfaction with their imamate. Rather, that is because his madhhab was that the mujtahid must follow his own ijtihad and it is not permissible for him to imitate another mujtahid, while the madhhab of Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf and Uthman was that the mujtahid may imitate another if he is more jurisprudentially knowledgeable, more learned in the ways of religion, and more insightful into the aspects of analogical reasoning, and that he should abandon his own ijtihad and opinion and follow the opinion of that one. And this difference remained among the imams of the religion and the jurists of the ummah. And the evidence that this was because of a difference between them on this issue and not because of opposition to them is that he pledged allegiance to Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) and believed in his imamate, and he used to assist him in his affairs. And the conclusion is that whoever rejects the caliphate of any of the Rightly Guided Caliphs or attributes to one of them what is not permissible or what necessitates a defect in his state, he is one who removes the consensus of the Companions from being a proof against him, and describes them as having agreed upon what is wrong and refraining from changing it. And most of what they narrate that necessitates a defect in one of them are reports established by solitary chains (aahad), and the whole matter depends on those who are not trustworthy, so it is not acceptable in the face of the consensus of the Companions. And much of what they narrate are things in which they have no proof, and they think, due to their ignorance of their exits and aspects, that it is a proof for them. And adhering to the consensus of the Companions is more deserving than following such aberrant reports that are outside [the accepted norms].
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection and provides a final defense. The author first narrates a report where Abd al-Rahman offered the caliphate to Ali on condition he rule by Quran, Sunnah, and the sira of Abu Bakr and Umar. Ali agreed to Quran and Sunnah but added 'and I exercise my own ijtihad,' while Uthman accepted the condition. The author argues this actually proves the soundness of the caliphate of Abu Bakr and Umar and the Companions' approval of them. He then explains that Ali's response is not evidence of opposition; rather, it reflects a known ijtihadi difference: Ali believed a mujtahid must follow his own ijtihad and cannot imitate, while Abd al-Rahman and Uthman held that a mujtahid may imitate a more knowledgeable one. This difference remained among scholars. The proof that it was not opposition is that Ali later pledged allegiance to Uthman and assisted him. The passage concludes by warning against those who reject any caliph or accuse them falsely: such people undermine the consensus of the Companions, rely on unreliable solitary narrations, and ignore clear consensus. Adhering to consensus is superior to following aberrant reports.

Key Terms
إجماع الصحابة
اجتهاد
تقليد
سيرة الشيخين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali's refusal to follow the sira shows he rejected Abu Bakr and Umar.
Correction: Ali's response was based on his ijtihad methodology; he followed them in practice and later pledged allegiance to Uthman.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why did Ali not accept the condition to follow the sira of Abu Bakr and Umar?
A) He disapproved of their rule
B) He believed a mujtahid must follow his own ijtihad
C) He was not aware of their sira
D) He was forced by his followers

Key Takeaway
Ali's conditional acceptance of the caliphate was due to his ijtihad methodology, not opposition to Abu Bakr and Umar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever resist following the way of the righteous predecessors (like the sira of the two shaykhs) because you prefer your own reasoning? What does this lesson teach you about the difference between legitimate methodological disagreement and disobedience?
?
The Companions differed on a matter of fiqh methodology yet remained united. Are you quick to label those who differ from you in secondary matters as deviant, or do you first seek to understand their legitimate reasons?
?
When you encounter a teaching from the Quran and Sunnah that conflicts with your personal preference, do you exercise ijtihad to reinterpret it, or do you submit? How does Ali's example of following his ijtihad within the bounds of the Quran and Sunnah guide you?

Defending the Caliphs Against Objections
Final defense and consensus argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses consensus of the Companions and the weakness of solitary narrations to defend the caliphs against sectarian objections.

Core Idea
Consensus of the Companions overrides weak solitary reports.

In the final part of this passage, the author provides a conclusive defense of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. He argues that those who reject any caliph or accuse them of wrong are undermining the consensus of the Companions, and that most reports used against the caliphs are unreliable. This lesson focuses on that final argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the evidence of Ali’s true stance
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'والدليل على أن ذلك كان لاختلاف'. Notice that the author provides a concrete proof: Ali pledged allegiance to Uthman and assisted him.
This shows that Ali’s earlier conditional response was not opposition.
2
Understand the general refutation
The author then broadens the argument: anyone who rejects or slanders a caliph is effectively rejecting the consensus of the Companions and relying on unreliable solitary reports.
3
Appreciate the source prioritization
The author concludes that adhering to the consensus is superior to following aberrant reports. This is a key principle in Sunni theology.
The passage is cut off at the end but the point is clear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a negative report about a respected scholar or leader, do you immediately accept it, or do you weigh it against the overwhelming consensus of their good character? How can you train yourself to prioritize established trust over isolated accusations?
?
Is there a person of authority in your life (parent, teacher, leader) whom you have judged based on a single report without considering the collective testimony of others about their integrity? What would it take for you to retract that judgment?
?
The passage says that those who attack the caliphs are actually accusing the entire generation of Companions of agreeing on a wrong. How does this make you reconsider the way you evaluate criticisms against well-known figures from Islamic history? Are you prepared to defend the consensus of the righteous against individual attacks?

4 min
Ali's Ijtihad Statement: Methodology, Not Opposition
Nasafi explains that Ali's ijtihad statement reflects methodological difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ali's statement about judging by ijtihad is not evidence of opposition to Abu Bakr and Umar, but rather reflects a difference in ijtihad methodology.

Core Idea
Ali's ijtihad statement shows methodological difference, not political opposition.

This lesson examines a frequently misunderstood statement of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). When Ali said he judges by the Book, Sunnah, and his own ijtihad, some have claimed it shows he rejected the authority of Abu Bakr and Umar. Imam al-Nasafi clarifies that this is not the case; rather, it shows a legitimate difference in how mujtahids should proceed. Let's explore the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the flow. Note that Nasafi begins by rejecting the claim that Ali's words imply opposition.
Pay attention to the two different madhhabs described.
2
Identify the two methodological positions
Identify Ali's view that a mujtahid must follow his own ijtihad, and the view of Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf and Uthman that taqlid of a more knowledgeable mujtahid is permissible.
This is the core of the explanation.
3
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how a difference in ijtihad methodology does not affect the legitimacy of the caliphate or the unity of the community.
Nasafi emphasizes that this difference remained among scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول علي الله الله : أحكم بكتاب الله وسنة رسوله (۱) عليه السلام وأجتهد رأيي، ليس بدليل على مخالفته لهما ومجانبته إياهما لما مر من الدلائل الدالة على متابعته إياهما ورضاه بإمامتهما، بل ذلك لأن مذهبه كان أن المجتهد يجب عليه اتباع اجتهاده، ولا يجوز له تقليد غير من المجتهدين، وكان مذهب عبد الرحمن بن عوف وعثمان أن المجتهد يجوز أن يقلد غيره إذا كان أفقه منه، وأعلم بطرق الدين وأبصر بوجوه المقاييس، وأن يترك اجتهاد نفسه ورأيه ويتبع رأي ذلك، وبقى هذا الخلاف في أئمة (۳) الدين وفقهاء الأمة.
And the statement of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'I judge by the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of His Messenger (peace be upon him) and I exert my opinion (ijtihad),' is not evidence of his opposition to them (Abu Bakr and Umar) and his distancing from them, due to what has passed of evidences indicating his following of them and his pleasure with their imamate. Rather, that is because his madhhab was that the mujtahid must follow his own ijtihad, and it is not permissible for him to imitate (taqlid) another mujtahid. And the madhhab of Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf and Uthman was that the mujtahid may imitate another if he is more knowledgeable than him, more learned in the ways of the religion, and more insightful in the aspects of analogies (qiyas), and that he should abandon his own ijtihad and opinion and follow the opinion of that one. And this difference remained among the imams of the religion and the jurists of the community.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage presents a definition-and-explanation of a historical argument. Imam al-Nasafi addresses a potential misunderstanding: the famous statement of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that he would judge by the Book, Sunnah, and his own ijtihad might be taken by some to indicate that he rejected the authority of Abu Bakr and Umar. The author clarifies that this is not the case. He begins by stating that Ali's words are not evidence of opposition, given the many proofs that show he followed them and accepted their imamate. The reason for Ali's statement is a difference in ijtihad methodology: Ali's school held that a mujtahid must follow his own reasoning and cannot imitate others, whereas the school of Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf and Uthman permitted taqlid of a more knowledgeable jurist. This methodological difference was a legitimate scholarly disagreement among the early imams and jurists, not a sign of dissent regarding the caliphate.

Key Terms
إمامة
اجتهاد
تقليد
مجتهد
صحابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali's statement that he judges by his ijtihad proves he opposed Abu Bakr and Umar.
Correction: Nasafi argues that Ali's statement reflects his madhhab that a mujtahid must follow his own ijtihad, and does not indicate opposition to the preceding caliphs.

Checkpoint
What does Imam al-Nasafi say is the real reason for Ali's statement about judging by his ijtihad?
A) Ali opposed the caliphate of Abu Bakr and Umar.
B) Ali believed a mujtahid must follow his own ijtihad and cannot imitate others.
C) Ali was unaware of the Sunnah.
D) Ali was forced to say that by his companions.

Key Takeaway
Ali's ijtihad statement is not a sign of opposition but a legitimate difference in jurisprudential methodology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim acting in a way you think is wrong, do you immediately assume they are opposing the truth? How can you instead follow the example of the Sahaba and assume good faith, seeking to understand their reasoning before judging? Will you commit to asking questions before forming conclusions?
?
The lesson shows that Ali and other Sahaba had legitimate differences in methodology. Think of a relationship where you have a disagreement with someone about a religious practice. Is it possible that both of you have valid reasons? Can you accept that difference without breaking the bond of brotherhood? What one step can you take today to heal that relationship?
?
Do you value your own opinion so much that you reject the ijtihad of others? This lesson teaches that even the greatest scholars (the Sahaba) had different approaches. Where in your life are you guilty of insisting that your way is the only way? How can you humble yourself and acknowledge the legitimacy of other valid positions?

Sahaba Consensus and the Rejection of Weak Narrations
Nasafi presents an argument defending the Rashidun caliphs against accusations, using the consensus of the Companions and critiquing weak narrations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that rejecting any Rashidun caliph goes against the definitive consensus of the Companions, and why isolated narrations cannot overturn that consensus.

Core Idea
The Sahaba consensus stands firm against weak narrations.

Nasafi defends the integrity of the Rashidun caliphs by appealing to the consensus of the Companions. He first shows that even differences among them did not imply opposition, then argues that any accusation against a caliph rests on unreliable isolated reports that cannot challenge the established consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let Nasafi's argument flow naturally.
Notice the two-part structure: daleel (evidence) then hukm (conclusion).
2
Identify the two parts
The passage begins with a specific evidence: Ali pledged allegiance to Uthman. Then it moves to a general conclusion about rejecting any caliph and the weakness of narrations.
Mark where 'والدليل' ends and 'والحاصل' begins.
3
Analyze the critique of narrations
Nasafi says these narrations are ahad (isolated) and rely on unreliable narrators. They cannot be accepted against the consensus of the Sahaba.
Consider why he emphasizes 'مقابلة إجماع الصحابة'.
4
Reflect on the priority of consensus
Ask yourself: why does Nasafi say consensus outweighs these narrations, especially for matters requiring knowledge ('ilm) rather than action?
Consensus itself is a source of certain knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أن ذلك كان لاختلاف فيما بينهما في هذه المسألة دون أن يرى على مخالفتهما : أنه بايع عثمان لله واعتقد إمامته، وكان يساعده في أموره. والحاصل أن كل من يأبى خلافة أحد من الخلفاء الراشدين أو ينسب واحدا منهم إلى ما لا يحل أو إلى ما يوجب قدحا في حاله، فهو ممن يزيل إجماع الصحابة عن كونه حجة عليه، ويصفهم بالإجماع على ما هو المنكر، والامتناع عن تغييره، وأكثر ما يروون ما يوجب قدحا في واحد منهم أخبار تثبت بطريق الآحاد، ومدار ذلك كله على من لا يوثق به فلا يكون مقبولاً بمقابلة إجماع الصحابة، وكثير مما يروون أشياء لا حجة لهم فيها، ويظنونها بجهالتهم بمخارجها ووجوهها، حجة لهم، والتمسك بإجماع الصحابة أولى من اتباع مثل تلك الروايات الشاذة الخارجة عن إجماع المسلمين خصوصا فيما الحاجة فيه إلى ثبوت العلم دون العمل، وهي موجبة للعلم وإن خلت عن معارضة الإجماع، فكيف وقد وردت بمخالفته.
And the evidence that this [difference] was about a disagreement between them in this matter, and not that he held opposition to them, is that he pledged allegiance to Uthman for Allah and believed in his imamate, and he assisted him in his affairs. The conclusion is that whoever rejects the caliphate of any of the Rightly Guided Caliphs or attributes to any of them what is not lawful or what necessitates a flaw in his condition, then he is one who removes the consensus (ijma) of the Companions from being a proof against him, and describes them as having agreed upon that which is objectionable, and refraining from changing it. Most of what they narrate that necessitates a flaw in any of them are reports established through isolated transmission (khabar al-ahad), and the entire matter relies on someone who is not trustworthy, so it is not acceptable in the face of the consensus of the Companions. Much of what they narrate are things in which they have no proof, and they think, due to their ignorance of their interpretations and aspects, that they are proof for them. Adhering to the consensus of the Companions is more worthy than following such anomalous narrations that depart from the consensus of the Muslims, especially in matters where the need is for the establishment of knowledge ('ilm) rather than action. And such [consensus] necessitates knowledge even if it were free from being opposed by consensus, so how [can it be ignored] when it has been reported in opposition to it?
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-conclusion argument. Imam al-Nasafi first provides evidence that Ali's differing methodology was not a sign of opposition: Ali pledged allegiance to Uthman, believed in his imamate, and assisted him. Then he draws a general conclusion. Anyone who rejects the caliphate of any Rashidun caliph or imputes wrongdoing to them is effectively dismissing the consensus of the Sahaba as a binding proof, and accusing them of agreeing upon something evil without changing it. Al-Nasafi then critiques the sources of such accusations: they rely on isolated reports (ahad) from untrustworthy narrators, which cannot stand against the established consensus. Moreover, many of these narrations are misunderstood due to ignorance of their proper interpretation. He emphasizes that adhering to the Sahaba's consensus is superior, especially when the issue requires certain knowledge ('ilm) rather than practical action. The consensus itself produces knowledge, and these narrations are not only weak but contradict that consensus.

Key Terms
إجماع الصحابة
خبر الآحاد
علم
خلافة
روافض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any narration that disparages a Rashidun caliph is valid and should be accepted.
Correction: Nasafi shows such narrations are isolated (ahad) and based on untrustworthy sources, and they contradict the definitive Sahaba consensus, so they are not acceptable.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the main problem with narrations that disparage a Rashidun caliph?
A) They are all fabricated.
B) They are isolated reports (ahad) from unreliable narrators and contradict the consensus of the Sahaba.
C) They are only about actions, not beliefs.
D) They are all from the Rafidah.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi asserts that the consensus of the Companions regarding the caliphs is decisive, and any isolated narrations to the contrary are invalid and cannot override that consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a criticism against one of the rightly guided caliphs, do you immediately question the source, or do you accept it without considering the consensus of the Companions? How will you train yourself to prioritize that consensus starting today?
?
If the consensus of the Companions is decisive and produces certain knowledge, what does that mean for any doubts you may have had about their integrity? Will you now actively seek to erase those doubts from your heart?
?
Are there any isolated narrations you have relied upon to form an opinion about a Companion? After this lesson, what concrete step will you take to re-evaluate that narration against the backdrop of the Sahaba's consensus?

Uthman's Authority in the Case of Ubaydullah ibn Umar
Nasafi refutes the claim that Uthman wrongly spared Ubaydullah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the accusation against Uthman for not executing Ubaydullah ibn Umar is invalid according to Islamic legal principles regarding the wali and Imam's authority.

Core Idea
The Imam has the authority to accept blood-money when the victim has no individual heir.

This lesson examines one of the criticisms leveled against Caliph Uthman for sparing Ubaydullah ibn Umar after he killed al-Hurmuzan. Nasafi demonstrates that this criticism stems from a misunderstanding of Islamic legal authority. In such cases, the Imam acts on behalf of the community and may choose execution or accept blood-money.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the accusation and Nasafi's response.
Pay attention to the legal reasoning.
2
Identify the legal basis
Note how Nasafi establishes that the community is the guardian (wali) because al-Hurmuzan had no heir, and the Imam has authority to decide.
The key concepts are wali ad-dam and diyah.
3
Reflect on the implication
Understand why this refutes the Rafidah's criticism and how it demonstrates the importance of knowing legal principles before criticizing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعض المطاعن التي وجهت لسيدنا عثمان والرد عليها فأما ما طعنت الروافض على عثمان الله أنه ترك قتل عبد الله بن عمر حين قتل الهرمزان (۲) لاتهامه إياه في قتل عمر الله، فطعن في غير مطعن وذلك لأن ولي الهرمزان جماعة المسلمين إذ لم يكن له وارث، والإمام هو القائم بأمور المسلمين فيكون هو المتصرف في استيفاء القتل (۳) أو (٤) العفو بالدية فمن طعن بذلك فلجهله طعن.
Some of the criticisms directed at our master Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) and the responses to them: As for what the Rafidah criticized Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) about, that he left off killing Abdullah ibn Umar when he killed al-Hurmuzan because he suspected him of being involved in the killing of Umar (may Allah be pleased with him), it is a criticism in something other than a valid criticism. That is because the guardian of al-Hurmuzan was the community of Muslims, since he had no heir, and the Imam is the one who manages the affairs of the Muslims, so he is the one in charge of executing the death penalty or pardoning by accepting blood-money. So whoever criticizes him for that, it is due to his ignorance.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. It begins with a heading introducing refutations of accusations against Uthman. The first accusation is from the Rafidah: that Uthman failed to execute Abdullah ibn Umar for killing al-Hurmuzan, who was suspected of involvement in the assassination of Umar. The answer: this is not a valid criticism because al-Hurmuzan had no heir, so the guardian (wali) is the community of Muslims. The Imam (Uthman) is the one who acts on behalf of the community, and he has the authority either to demand execution or to accept blood-money as a settlement. Therefore, his decision to spare Abdullah ibn Umar (by accepting diyah) was within his rights. The one who criticizes this does so out of ignorance of Islamic legal principles.

Key Terms
ولي الدم
دية
إمام
روافض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Uthman arbitrarily pardoned a murderer without legal basis.
Correction: Nasafi shows he had legal authority as Imam and the victim had no individual heir, so the community was the wali and the Imam could choose diyah.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi consider the accusation about Uthman not killing Abdullah ibn Umar to be invalid?
A) Because Abdullah ibn Umar was innocent.
B) Because al-Hurmuzan had no heir, so the Imam had authority to accept blood-money.
C) Because Uthman was not the caliph at that time.
D) Because Abdullah ibn Umar had a personal vendetta.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the accusation by showing that when a victim has no heir, the Imam has authority to accept blood-money, and Uthman's action was legally sound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever criticized a scholar or leader without fully understanding the legal basis of their decision? Will you commit to seeking knowledge before passing judgment?
?
This lesson shows that when a victim has no heir, the entire community is their guardian. How does this expand your sense of responsibility towards those who have no one to defend them?
?
If you were in Uthman's position, trusting your authority in a complex legal situation, would you have the courage to follow the law even if it seemed unpopular? What would you need to strengthen that trust?

Refutation of the Accusation about Returning al-Hakam
Nasafi refutes the accusation against Uthman by multiple reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's reasoning for why Uthman's return of al-Hakam was justified, based on prophetic permission, cessation of cause, and duty of kinship.

Core Idea
Uthman returned al-Hakam with the Prophet's permission and for a valid reason.

This lesson addresses a historical accusation against Caliph Uthman: that he reinstated a man the Prophet himself had exiled. To defend Uthman, Nasafi presents a multi-layered argument involving prophetic permission, the caliph's personal knowledge, and moral duties. We will examine each part of this defense.

Guided Path
1
Understand the accusation
Read the passage and note the charge: Uthman returned al-Hakam ibn al-As, who was exiled by the Prophet.
The term 'طريد' means exile.
2
First justification: prophetic permission
Nasafi reports that Uthman claimed he sought and obtained the Prophet's permission to return al-Hakam.
This is the primary defense.
3
Failure to act under predecessors
Uthman informed Abu Bakr and Umar, but they required another witness. Uthman did not act until he became caliph himself.
This shows Uthman did not act unilaterally.
4
Uthman's own rule: multiple grounds
When Uthman became caliph, he ruled based on his knowledge of the permission, the original cause for exile had ended, and maintaining kinship ties was obligatory.
The return was thus justified by three converging reasons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا: أنه رد الحكم بن العاص - طريد رسول الله عليه السلام - إلى المدينة، فهو أيضا فاسد، فإن عثمان الله أخبره أنه كان استأذن النبي عليه السلام في رده فأذن له، وقد كان أخبر بذلك أبا بكر الله فطالبه بشاهد آخر معه ليرده، وكذا فعل عمر الله، فلما وُلِّيَ هو بنفسه حكم بعلمه، على أن المعنى الذي كان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أخرجه له قد كان ارتفع وكان عما له، وكانت صلة قرابته واجبة عليه فرده صلة للقرابة مع علمه بزوال المعنى الموجب لطرده وإخراجه.
And what they said: that he returned al-Hakam ibn al-As—the exile of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him)—to Medina, that too is corrupt. For Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) informed him that he had sought permission from the Prophet (peace be upon him) to return him, and he allowed him. And he had informed Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with him) about that, but he asked him for another witness with him in order to return him, and similarly Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) did. So when he himself became caliph, he ruled based on his own knowledge. Moreover, the reason for which the Prophet (peace be upon him) had exiled him had ceased, and there was an obligation upon him to maintain ties of kinship, so he returned him as an act of kinship, with his knowledge of the removal of the reason that necessitated his expulsion and banishment.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer. The accusation is that Uthman wrongfully brought back al-Hakam ibn al-As, whom the Prophet had exiled from Medina. The answer is presented in several steps. First, Uthman claimed that he had sought the Prophet's permission to return al-Hakam, and the Prophet granted it. Second, he had informed Abu Bakr of this, but Abu Bakr required another witness; similarly Umar did not act on it. Third, when Uthman became caliph, he acted based on his own knowledge of that permission. Fourth, the original reason for exile (some offense) had ceased, and additionally, maintaining kinship ties (silat al-rahim) was obligatory, so Uthman returned him as a fulfillment of that duty, knowing that the cause of expulsion was no longer present. Thus, the return was justified.

Key Terms
طريد
صلة الرحم
علم الإمام
استئذان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Uthman acted against the Prophet's explicit decision by returning al-Hakam.
Correction: Nasafi shows that Uthman had the Prophet's permission to return him, and the original cause for exile had ceased, making the return valid.

Checkpoint
What was one of the justifications offered by Nasafi for Uthman returning al-Hakam?
A) Uthman was related to al-Hakam and did not know the Prophet's decree.
B) The Prophet had given Uthman permission to return al-Hakam.
C) Abu Bakr and Umar also returned him.
D) Al-Hakam was never exiled.

Key Takeaway
Uthman's return of al-Hakam was justified by three arguments: prior prophetic permission, end of the cause for exile, and the duty of maintaining family ties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a grievance against someone, do you check whether the reason for that grievance still exists today? Uthman's example shows that the cause of exile had ceased. What past offense are you still holding onto that may no longer be valid? Will you let it go?
?
Uthman returned al-Hakam partly out of kinship obligation. Is there a family member you have distanced yourself from due to old conflicts, even though the cause may have ended? What would it take for you to restore that relationship?
?
Uthman acted on his firsthand knowledge of the Prophet's permission even though others required a second witness. Are there times when you rely on the opinion of others rather than acting on what you know to be true from reliable knowledge? What would it cost you to follow your certain knowledge with courage?

Defense of Uthman: The Governors' Betrayal
Nasafi refutes the claim that Uthman is blameworthy for his governors' misconduct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi defends Uthman by showing that a leader's integrity is not undermined by the misconduct of subordinates when corrective action is taken.

Core Idea
A leader's integrity is judged by their own actions, not the betrayals of appointed officials.

Nasafi continues his refutation of criticisms against Uthman by addressing the claim that some of Uthman's governors were corrupt. Critics argue this shows Uthman had poor judgment. But Nasafi counters that Uthman dismissed these governors as soon as their misconduct was known, proving his own integrity. In this passage, we see the principle that a leader is responsible for their own response, not for the hidden crimes of others.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice how Nasafi structures his argument: accusation, answer, and example.
The text has a double 'على' which may be a scribal repetition.
2
Identify the accusation and answer
Find the claim (that some governors betrayed) and Nasafi's response (they were dismissed). Understand that the dismissal shows Uthman's swift action.
3
Examine the example
Look at the example of al-Qa'qa' ibn Shur. He was appointed, took wealth, and joined Mu'awiyah. Uthman dismissed him, proving the point.
This example reinforces that the governor's crime was personal, not a reflection on Uthman.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how this principle applies: a leader is not responsible for the hidden betrayal of subordinates if they act justly when it is revealed.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أن بعض عماله خانوا وظهرت منهم أمور مستكرهة فاسد أيضا، لأنه لما ظهر ذلك من ولاته عزلهم، وذلك لا يوجب قدحا فيه ولا وهنا في حاله، فإن القعقاع بن شور ولاه على على ميسان (۹) ، فأخذ أموالها ولحق بمعاوية (٦)
And what they claim that some of his governors betrayed and objectionable matters appeared from them is also corrupt, because when that appeared from his governors, he dismissed them. And that does not necessitate a flaw in him nor weakness in his condition. For indeed, al-Qa'qa' ibn Shur—he appointed him over Maysan—he took its wealth and joined Mu'awiyah (may Allah be pleased with him).
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is the third objection-and-answer. The accusation is that some of Uthman's governors were corrupt or betrayed their trust, implying that Uthman was at fault. The answer: as soon as such misconduct became known, Uthman dismissed those governors. This shows that he acted responsibly; their personal betrayal does not reflect on him. The example given is al-Qa'qa' ibn Shur, whom Uthman appointed over Maysan, but he then absconded with public funds and joined Mu'awiyah. Uthman's action of dismissal demonstrates his integrity, and the governor's crime is his own, not the caliph's.

Key Terms
عمال
خيانة
عزل
ولاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a leader appoints someone who later becomes corrupt, the leader is automatically blameworthy.
Correction: Uthman dismissed corrupt governors as soon as their misconduct was known, showing that a leader is only blameworthy if they condone or ignore wrongdoing.

Checkpoint
How did Uthman respond when his governors betrayed according to Nasafi?
A) He ignored the reports.
B) He dismissed them immediately.
C) He punished only those who spoke out.
D) He resigned as caliph.

Key Takeaway
Uthman's dismissal of corrupt governors proves that a leader's integrity is not compromised by the personal failings of subordinates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a person you trusted and appointed lets you down, is your first reaction to defend yourself or to take corrective action like Uthman did? What does that reveal about your heart?
?
Have you ever shifted blame for a mistake onto someone else, even though you were ultimately responsible? How can Uthman's example of not being weakened by the betrayal of his governors challenge you to own your own actions?
?
If you lead a team or family, do you have the courage to remove someone who betrays trust, even if it costs you reputation or relationships? What is holding you back from that integrity?

Why Uthman Burned the Qurans
Author refutes accusation against Uthman and justifies the burning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasoning behind Caliph Uthman's burning of the Quran copies and why it is considered a legitimate act of preservation.

Core Idea
Uthman burned the Quran to preserve unity, not to destroy revelation.

The accusation that Caliph Uthman burned the Quran is often misunderstood as an act of arrogance or alteration. This passage from a classic creedal text provides a clear defense, explaining the motive behind the action and drawing an analogy to a common educational practice. Let's examine the source to understand the rationale.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's argument.
Notice the use of the analogy at the end.
2
Identify the accusation and the response
The passage begins with the accusation and immediately responds with the reason: to prevent recitation of non-consensus versions. Understand why this reason addresses the accusation.
The key point is the collective agreement of the Companions.
3
Reflect on the analogy
The analogy to washing school tablets is meant to show that actions for a beneficial purpose are permissible. How does this relate to Uthman's action?
Think about the benefit of preserving a unified text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أنه أحرق المصاحف فذلك لئلا يقرأ إنسان بغير ما أجمعت الصحابة على ثبوته (ومثل ذلك لمصلحة ما، لا يكون منكرا كغسل الألواح في المكاتب).
And what they claim that he burned the Qurans, that was so that no one would recite according to other than what the Companions agreed upon its authenticity, and something like that for a beneficial purpose is not reprehensible, like washing the tablets in the school.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the accusation that Caliph Uthman ibn Affan burned the Quran. The author states that the burning was done for a valid reason: to ensure that people read only according to the consensus of the Companions on the authentic text. He analogizes it to the common practice of washing writing tablets in a school after the lesson is learned — it is a permissible act for a beneficial purpose.

Key Terms
المصاحف
الصحابة
المصلحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Uthman burned the Quran out of arrogance or to alter the text.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the burning was to preserve the authentic version agreed upon by the Companions, not to change or destroy revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what was the purpose of Uthman burning the Qurans?
A) To destroy evidence of errors
B) To prevent recitation of any version not agreed upon by the Companions
C) To assert his authority
D) To punish those who had different readings

Key Takeaway
Uthman's burning of variant Quran copies was a legitimate act to safeguard the unified, authentic text, just as washing school tablets is permissible for a beneficial purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about controversial actions of respected figures like Uthman, do you rush to judge or seek the wisdom behind the act? This lesson reveals a noble intention. How can you train yourself to assume good faith in others, especially when you don't understand their choices?
?
The analogy of washing tablets shows that discarding something after its benefit is taken is permissible. What attachments—whether habits, opinions, or possessions—are you clinging to long after they've served their purpose? Are you willing to 'wash the tablets' for a greater spiritual benefit?
?
Uthman sacrificed many copies to preserve the authentic text. Is there a unity or truth in your community that requires you to let go of something you hold dear? What would it cost you, and are you prepared to pay that price?

Refuting the Claims of Uthman Striking Ammar
Author rejects as unsubstantiated the story of Uthman striking Ammar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes two specific sectarian accusations using textual evidence and rational argument.

Core Idea
Two accusations, one refutation: neither Uthman struck Ammar nor did Ammar call him a disbeliever.

In this passage, the author addresses a common sectarian narrative that paints early companions in a negative light. He directly refutes two claims: that Uthman physically struck Ammar, and that Ammar declared Uthman a disbeliever. Using reports from Ali and his son Hasan, the author shows these stories are unsubstantiated and even contradicted by the companions themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's points.
Notice the author uses words like 'غير ثابت' (not established) and 'كذب' (lie) to dismiss the claims.
2
Identify the two refuted claims
Find the two specific accusations that the author rejects. One is about Uthman's action, the other about Ammar's statement.
The first claim is that Uthman struck Ammar; the second is that Ammar said 'we killed him as a disbeliever'.
3
Examine the supporting evidence
The author cites reports from Ali and al-Hasan ibn Ali to counter the accusations. Note how Ali's question to Ammar exposes the absurdity of the claim.
Ammar's silence is presented as implicit agreement with Ali's rebuke.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أنه ضرب عمارا [؟] فغير ثابت وما يزعمون أن عمارا قال: قتلناه كافرًا، كذب على عمار لم يثبت ذلك عنه، وروي أن عليا أنكر ذلك على عمار، وكذا الحسن بن علي حين روى أن عليا قال له: أتكفر برب كان يؤمن به عثمان؟ فسكت عمار.
And what they claim that he struck Ammar [?] is not established, and what they claim that Ammar said: 'We killed him as a disbeliever' is a lie upon Ammar, not established from him, and it is narrated that Ali disapproved of that upon Ammar, and likewise al-Hasan ibn Ali when it was narrated that Ali said to him: 'Do you disbelieve in a Lord in whom Uthman believed?' So Ammar remained silent.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage refutes two accusations: that Uthman physically struck Ammar, and that Ammar declared Uthman a disbeliever after killing him. The author declares both unsubstantiated. He then cites a report where Ali rebuked Ammar for something (presumably the statement), and another where Ali's son Hasan narrated that Ali asked Ammar: 'Do you disbelieve in a Lord in whom Uthman believed?' — implying the accusation is absurd. Ammar's silence is presented as a sign of his agreement or concession.

Key Terms
عمار
علي
كذب
ثابت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ammar ibn Yasir said 'We killed Uthman as a disbeliever' after Uthman's death.
Correction: The author states this is a lie upon Ammar, not established from him. He cites Ali's rebuke and Ammar's silence as evidence that this statement is false.

Checkpoint
How does the author describe the claim that Ammar said 'We killed him as a disbeliever'?
A) True but misreported
B) A lie upon Ammar that is not established
C) A statement that some scholars accept
D) A report that is weakly authentic

Key Takeaway
The author firmly rejects sectarian claims about Uthman and Ammar, emphasizing that they are baseless and contradicted by reliable reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a harmful rumor about someone without demanding evidence? How will this lesson's insistence on substantiation change your approach when you hear accusations against others?
?
When someone claims a fellow Muslim has committed disbelief, does your heart lean towards acceptance or verification? What would you learn from Ammar's silence in response to Ali's rebuke?
?
Are there any stories about the early Muslims that you have believed without checking their authenticity? Will you commit to investigating their sources before passing them on, knowing that false narratives can cause division?

The Claim of Uthman Exiling Abu Dharr is Not Established
Author denies the exile story and provides an alternative justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the accusation that Uthman exiled Abu Dharr is not historically established and even if it were, it could be justified.

Core Idea
The exile story is not proven, and even if true, it was for a valid reason.

The author begins by refuting the claim that Uthman exiled Abu Dharr. He first emphasizes that the story is not reliably transmitted, citing the testimony of al-Hasan al-Basri. Then he argues that even if it were true, it could be interpreted as a legitimate administrative decision for the greater good of the community.

Guided Path
1
Read the denial by al-Hasan al-Basri
Notice how al-Hasan al-Basri sharply denies that Uthman expelled Abu Dharr, calling those who claim so liars. His companions are confused, but he explains that the questioner distorted the wording.
The word 'distortion' (تصحيف) suggests a misunderstanding.
2
Understand the hypothetical justification
Even if the claim were true, it would be permissible because Uthman saw a benefit. The text explains Abu Dharr's harsh criticism was undermining the caliphate's prestige, so sending him away was a practical solution.
This is a common juristic principle: a leader may take necessary measures.
3
Reflect on the lessons for judging historical figures
Ask yourself: How does this passage teach us to interpret the actions of the Companions? What principles should guide our judgment?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أنه نفى أبا ذر ليس بثابت أيضا، وروى أن الحسن البصري كان جالسا في مجلسه فدخل عليه رجل فقال: أعثمان أخرج أبا ذر؟ فقال الحسن: لا. كذبوا. فتعجب أصحابه من ذلك وقالوا له: ما معنى ما دار بينكما من الكلام؟ فقال: إنه سأل على طريق التصحيف أعثمان أخرج أبا ذر؟ فقلت: لا، ولو ثبت لكان من الجائز أنه أخرجه لمصلحة رآها في ذلك. وروى أن أبا ذر كان رجلا متزهدا، وكان عثمان موسرا وكان يعيش عيش الأغنياء، وكان أبو ذر يطلب منه أن يقتدي بأبي بكر وعمر - رضي الله عنهما - في رفض الزينة ومجانبة الشبهات وكان يخاشنه في الكلام في ذلك المعنى على وجه كان يذهب بهيبة الخلافة، فرأى المصلحة في أن بعثه إلى الربذة، ولا عيب في هذا، وروي أن معاوية كتب إلى عثمان يشكو أبا ذر فكتب عثمان إلى أبي ذر يستقدمه إلى المدينة، فأبى أبو ذر، وقال: سمعت رسول الله عليه السلام يقول: 'إذا بلغت عمارة المدينة إلى موضع كذا فاخرج عنها' وقد بلغت العمارة ذلك الموضع فخيره عثمان أي البلاد أحب إليه؟ فاختار الربذة، فقال له: فاخرج إليها، ولو لم يرو شيء من هذه الوجوه لكان الواجب حمل أمر عثمان مع زهده وورعه وجلال قدره في الدين في كونه من الذين هاجروا هجرتين، وختنا لرسول الله على الابنتين وإنفاقه في نصرة الدين وتجهيز جيش المسلمين كل نفيس وخطير من الأموال ومضنون به من النعم، وكونه من المبشرين بالجنة، وقول النبي عليه السلام فيه عثمان: 'أخي ورفيقي في الجنة' وقوله عليه السلام - لما ستر ركبته عند مجيء عثمان -: 'ألا أستحي ممن تستحي منه ملائكة السماء'، وقوله عليه السلام فيه وفي علي رضي الله عنهما لما أتياه في شيء: 'هكذا تدخلان الجنة لا يحبكما إلا مؤمن ولا يبغضكما إلا منافق' وروي أنه عليه السلام قال...
And what they claim that he exiled Abu Dharr is also not established, and it is narrated that al-Hasan al-Basri was sitting in his gathering when a man entered and said: 'Did Uthman expel Abu Dharr?' So al-Hasan said: 'No. They lied.' His companions were astonished at that and said to him: 'What is the meaning of what passed between you in speech?' He said: 'He asked by way of distortion: "Did Uthman expel Abu Dharr?" So I said: "No." And even if it were established, it would be permissible that he expelled him for a benefit he saw in that.' And it is narrated that Abu Dharr was a man of asceticism, and Uthman was wealthy and used to live the life of the rich, and Abu Dharr used to ask him to follow Abu Bakr and Umar - may Allah be pleased with them - in rejecting adornment and avoiding doubtful matters, and he used to speak harshly to him about that meaning in a way that undermined the prestige of the caliphate, so he saw the benefit in sending him to al-Rabadha, and there is no fault in this. And it is narrated that Muawiya wrote to Uthman complaining about Abu Dharr, so Uthman wrote to Abu Dharr summoning him to Medina, but Abu Dharr refused and said: 'I heard the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, say: "When the building of Medina reaches such-and-such a place, then leave it" and the building had reached that place.' So Uthman gave him choice of which land he liked best, he chose al-Rabadha, so he said to him: 'Go out to it.' And even if nothing of these accounts were narrated, it would be obligatory to interpret Uthman's affair with his asceticism, piety, and great standing in religion, being among those who emigrated twice, and the son-in-law of the Messenger of Allah to both his daughters, and his spending in supporting the religion and equipping the army of the Muslims with every precious and valuable thing from wealth and treasured blessings, and being among those given glad tidings of Paradise, and the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him, about Uthman: 'He is my brother and companion in Paradise', and his saying, peace be upon him - when he covered his knee at the coming of Uthman -: 'Should I not feel shy of one whom the angels of heaven feel shy?', and his saying, peace be upon him, about him and Ali, may Allah be pleased with them both, when they came to him regarding something: 'Thus you two will enter Paradise. None loves you except a believer and none hates you except a hypocrite', and it is narrated that he, peace be upon him, said...
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive defense of Uthman against the accusation of unjustly exiling Abu Dharr. The author presents multiple arguments: 1) The story itself is not established; he cites al-Hasan al-Basri denying it and explaining that the questioner 'distorted' the word. 2) Even if it were true, it could have been for a valid reason: Abu Dharr was ascetic and bluntly criticized Uthman's wealth and lifestyle, which was seen to undermine the caliphate's dignity, so Uthman sent him away for the greater good. 3) Another narration says Abu Dharr himself refused to stay in Medina due to a hadith from the Prophet about leaving Medina when the city expands, and Uthman simply let him choose a place. 4) Finally, even if none of these explanations existed, Uthman's impeccable virtues—his early migration, his being the Prophet's son-in-law twice, his immense spending for Islam, his being one of the ten promised Paradise, and the Prophet's high praise of him—would oblige us to interpret his actions in the best possible light.

Key Terms
أبو ذر
عثمان
الحسن البصري
الربذة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Uthman exiled Abu Dharr out of personal enmity or injustice.
Correction: The author shows the story is not reliably transmitted, and if it occurred, it was for a legitimate public benefit.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why did al-Hasan al-Basri deny that Uthman expelled Abu Dharr?
A) He had no knowledge of the event
B) The questioner distorted the wording
C) He wanted to protect Uthman's reputation
D) He was afraid of Uthman

Key Takeaway
The accusation of Uthman exiling Abu Dharr is not proven, and even if it were, it could be for a valid reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear an accusation against a person of authority—whether a leader, scholar, or even a family member—do you instinctively believe it, or do you pause to verify the truth? What can you learn from al-Hasan al-Basri's careful, cautious response to the question about Uthman?
?
Have you ever spoken harshly to someone in authority, thinking you were advising them, only to realize later that your words damaged your relationship and your own credibility? How can you combine sincere advice with the respect that positions of authority deserve?
?
The author says Uthman's action was for a benefit he saw. Think of a time when you judged someone's action as wrong without knowing the full picture. Will you commit to giving Muslims—especially those you disagree with—the benefit of the doubt, as this lesson teaches?

Alternative Narratives and Uthman's Virtues
Author gives an alternative narration and argues from Uthman's virtues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative account where Abu Dharr chose to leave Medina and the argument that Uthman's virtues require us to interpret his actions positively.

Core Idea
Abu Dharr wanted to leave due to a hadith, and Uthman's virtues demand good assumptions.

The author continues by presenting another tradition: Abu Dharr himself refused to stay in Medina because the Prophet had foretold the city's expansion and commanded leaving. Uthman simply gave him a choice of destination. Finally, even without any external justification, we must interpret Uthman's actions favorably because of his impeccable character and the Prophet's praises.

Guided Path
1
Read the account of Muawiya's complaint
Notice that Muawiya wrote to Uthman complaining about Abu Dharr. Instead of punishing him, Uthman summoned Abu Dharr to Medina and let him choose.
2
Understand Abu Dharr's refusal
Abu Dharr cited a hadith about leaving Medina when it expands. This shows his departure was voluntary, not a forced exile.
3
Appreciate the argument from Uthman's virtues
Even without these narrations, Uthman's high status in Islam—his early migration, being the Prophet's son-in-law, his generosity, and being promised Paradise—obliges us to interpret his actions in the best light.
This is a principled approach to early Muslim history.

?
Abu Dharr relied on a specific prophetic hadith to guide his decision. In your own life, when facing a moral dilemma, do you genuinely seek divine guidance from revelation, or do you default to personal preference? What is one concrete step you can take this week to make the Qur'an and Sunnah your primary compass?
